I think that U.S. interest in protecting Taiwan will end once they repatriate fab capability.  At that point, however, they won't export chip technology to Taiwan.  But it is possible that Taiwan could continue being a chip power house for other nations, assuming that other nations aren't scared off or impeded by a blockade.  It is also possible that China will throw a lot of money at Taiwan to make use of its chip technology, but in order for Taiwan to play ball, they would have to burn bridges with the U.S.  So the U.S. might still have incentive to protect Taiwan.  If U.S. fabs pull ahead enough, and if Taiwan's falls behind, that incentive may wane.  Perhaps that's where China steps in with a lot of money to keep Taiwan's capability modernized.

A microchip is not really like a brain at all except that it is structured like a network.  The microchip is a series of logic stages synchronized by a clock.  You can chain logic gates (ands, ors, nots) to make more complicated things like adders and subtractors.  Really a computer is designed to do a series of things in a sequence to perform a more complicated task.

You're not wrong about silicon (chips) being in most modern devices and appliances but that's not really relevant to the point. Those low end chips are not what is being contested. There are a wide variety of companies with the knowledge and capability to manufacture low end chips. No one can or is trying to prevent China from acquiring low end chips. It's the cutting edge, high end chips manufactured with the latest process nodes that are guarded, such as those manufactured by TSMC, Intel, and Samsung. There are a slew of other fabs that manufacture less exciting commodity grade chips and they will continue to manufacture them and circulate them around the globe for use in everything from appliances, to cars, to toys, to medical equipment.

Even I (not an expert) know that aircraft, aviation, missiles, etc. do not require high process chips because of the high requirement for reliability and anti-interference. Most military chips are manufactured in the 65-120 nm process (also in the United States), and many countries can produce them without the need for "American technology".   The so-called "China uses American technology to enhance its military strength against the United States" is entirely an excuse. Firstly, military chips are not American technology, secondly, Chinese troops are not deployed overseas to "against the US". Finally, American military equipment also uses a large amount of alloys, materials, and technology from China.   The advanced chips purchased by China are fully used for commercial activities. Limiting China's access to, or even independent manufacturing of, advanced chips is primarily aimed at maintaining the advantages in next-generation industries, artificial intelligence, and other fields, which completely violates the principle of free market competition. This will greatly disrupt global supply chains, raise product prices, and ultimately destroy high-tech companies in the United States.ğŸ™„

But I do think that describing the Chinese Mainland and Taiwan as just â€˜neighborsâ€™ or two nations that had a bit of a history lacks the nuances the situation deserves. It is much more akin to â€˜estranged siblingsâ€™ that are each associated with some very dangerous people.   While the island of Taiwan along with Kinmen and Matsu were never absorbed back into PRC, it never officially claimed independence from Beijing either, which leaves it is the politically ambiguous state that inherits the name Republic of China (the flag you used for the map), with a government derived from the nationalist era of China which never officially ended the Chinese Civil War with the mainland government.  Taiwan has enjoyed and suffered a lot of unique situations due to this political limbo. And while with its own de facto government and military, it officially does not have diplomatic ties with any country on a national level, which makes it all the more sensitive as the center of geopolitical tension between the US and China when sovereignty comes into question.  Were it just another independent and legally recognized nation like, say, Singapore, then the situation would not be as delicate as it is. Just thought Iâ€™d mention it as it is oft taken as fact that Taiwan is â€˜just another countryâ€™ when it comes to those discussions, a sentiment that is no doubt very much aligned with the interest of the US, who loves to present it as an uncontested fact of life, but is not the entire truth.

The US blockade of high technology is only to slow down China's economic development, and preventing China's military development is just an excuse. In fact, China's military has been blocked by the United States for decades, but now it is the world's second or third largest. The blockade is useless. If the blockade worked, China would not have new missiles and large destroyers. Similarly, the blockade will only make China's chip industry rise more rapidly, just like military blockade. Thanks to Biden. Without Biden's chip blockade, China's high-tech industry would never have known that the United States would actually block chips, nor would it have the urgent need for the rise of the domestic chip industry as it does now, nor would it have adopted domestically produced chips that were low-end at the time. Now, Chinese high-tech companies are forced to choose low-efficiency but expensive domestic chips, which will inevitably lead to the rapid growth of the chip industry. Even if the United States reopens the Chinese market with low-priced, high-performance chips in the future, Chinese companies will not simply follow suit. They will inevitably retain a portion of the profits for domestic chips to ensure that they are no longer restrained by the US chip blockade.

What? This is some of the stupidest propaganda and lazy reporting on the issue. Chinese hypersonic missiles don't use any american micro chips. In fact, their missiles have nothing to do with the chip conflict. All Chinese military chips are produced in-house by the PLA and are much bigger than the nano-sized chips used for phones. What makes Chinese missiles ahead of american missiles are not chips, but aerodynamics and wind tunnels.   Wind tunnels allow China to create simulation of high altitude environment in order to test and iterate the aerodynamics of their missiles at a very low cost. The wind tunnels require infrastructure prowess to build. This is why China is way ahead of america. USA doesn't have advanced wind tunnels like China has. The only way to test and gather real data for their missiles is by firing a real missile or prototype in the atmosphere, which takes a lot of time and huge costs.  The reason why usa is cutting China off from the chips supply is because this is the area of tech that usa has the highest chance to win in the competition, whereas China is already winning on a lot of other things, such as infrastructure engineering, telecommunication, EV, etc. The chip blockade is usa's last chance to keep China from surpassing.

The Trilateral commission meeting held in Tokyo on 19 Nov 2022, consisting of invitation-only elites Asia Pacific group made of politicians, Finance minister officials, scholars, some members of the EU and NATO.  Interestingly the administration of U.S president Joe Biden, in it's first national security strategy released in October, the administration noted that China is the "only competitor with both the intent to reshape the international order and increasingly, the economic, diplomatic, military and technological power to do so."     If this Tokyo gathering showcased anything, it was that the Asia Pacific group is concerned about the intensifying competition between U.S and China, makes sense as China is extremely crucial for Asia, even though China seemingly isn't trusted. The discussion went further than geopolitics into economics as a South Korean economist raised a point that most countries are battling with now, that his country would inevitably have to choose between U.S or China for tech purposes. "There is an increasing number of dual purpose products in the manufacturing sector" he said, "Therefore if you want to stay ahead in competitiveness, you have to choose which tech ecosystem you belong to. The choice that we are pressured into is not politics but also very much economic pressure."  A fascinating discussion, you should check it out.

16:19, While it is true that the transistor density is still increasing, they aren't really getting smaller, 7nm, 5nm, 2nm etc. are just process names, nothing in there is actually this size. Transistors are not 1-Dimensional anyways, so the size used to be the length of the Gate of the transistor but that is no longer the case.  Good video where der8auer shows electronmicroscope shots of modern transistors: https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TtuUANbaEFI
Just to be clear, China can make 14nm chips, and most, in not all, military equipments NEEDS to thicker transistors (their satellites use 100+nm chips). This is due to fact that they need to be reliable and nearly resistant to all electronic interferences. They can either use good shielding or less shielding with "thicker" chips. American satellites use 100+nm chips as well ( according to the chips used on the perseverance Mars rover). That why I think, chips or not, China will have hypersonic weapons. And chips will not *directly relate to military power. Taiwan produce 3nm chips, but their weapon systems are so out dated that most barely work (according to Taiwan reserve training videos). Also, since America is big and power in terms of chips, then why do you think they don't even have a working demonstration vehicle for hypersonics?ğŸ˜…

Uhh... as a Taiwanese... Johnny made it sound like the situation is such a planned event from the Taiwanese government.  But the truth is (almost always) somewhere in the middle.  Honestly it's half planned, and half by accident that Taiwan got this "silicon shield", which the name is not even a thing until like 2021/22ish. It's also a really lucky half accident that TSMC didn't move to China, when in the late 90s to early 2000s Taiwan lost a lot of manufacturing to China.  The story goes that the other major fab MediaTek (under UMC) made the move to go to China, but the Taiwanese president at the time was able to convinced TSMC to wait first and see what happened.  Now 20 years later, the ex-president of UMC publicly expressing regrets moving to China, and TSMC becomes the head of the industry in Taiwan. There is also a whole saga of how China wasted billions of dollar trying to catch up and in the process poached quite a bit of high lvl management/engineers from TSMC/Taiwan, and all that effort down the drain... but that's a story for another day

16:15 That is not accurate, modern process nodes like IBMs 2nm do not actually give you any physical length. This used to be the case down to roughly 28nm, where the number gives you the gate-width of a transistor. More modern process nodes do not necessarily scale by making the gate all that much narrower, instead they scale by decreasing the distance between transistors, their height etc. Basically they just use other means of scalling but still use the old gate-width naming scheme so it is easier to roughly understand how dense and energy efficient a given process is.  The IBM 2nm process actually has a gate-width of roughly 12nm, so the virus is actually a very appropriate size comparison.

Finally Johnny talking about something I'm intimately familiar with! One thing to mention is that TSMC is building fabs in the US to help diversify. But as you might guess, building a fab is absurdly expensive, and takes a long time. Their Arizona fab alone is expected to cost $12B. Another thing to mention is that a large number of chips on older process nodes are already manufactured in the US (by Texas Instruments for example), and these are all the little "glue logic" chips that are around the fancy chips. These nodes have seen reductions in capacity as fabs have moved onto the newer (and higher profit margin) nodes which was a contributing factor in the current chip shortage. This is a very good video from US perspective, however it is a bit off on the reality in China. There are always two sides of the story. The video totally ignores the fact that China has the ability to manufacture his own advanced chips, even though not as advanced as US. China also has his own Fabs, EDA companies, equipment manufacturers and material supply chains. China's latest 5-year plan is to achieve intelligent manufacturing by 2025, and 10-year plan is to achieve 70% chip self-reliance by 2030. These plans are made even before the chip war. Nowadays China uses his own indigenous chips and Operating Systems in their supercomputers, satellites, space station, militaries and other key industries. By the end of 2022, China achieves 25% chip self-reliance. Yes, chip war makes it difficult for China and prolong the process to achieve self-reliance. However, even without the chip war, China is intended to change the course of chip manufacturing and pursuing self-reliance anyway as planned. The war just makes China going full throttle in indigenous semiconductor industry.

Correction: IBM's 2nm transistors are not actually 2nm big. The name is just purely marketing and that's been the case for these processes for awhile. The physical limit of transistors is around 5nm because around that scale you get the effects of quantum tunnelling happening making the transistor useless. The actual dimensions are three transistors stacked 75 nm high, and 40 nm wide. Bit disappointed this was missed

Great video on this chip issue between US and China. I do want to add to the points presented in this video that this chip war isn't all for geopolitics, it does have a business case as well. For years, Chinese government has been actively facilitating intellectual theft of foreign technology, usually in violation of their own laws or WTO rules. It is common knowledge in China that intellectual property rights are rarely protected, and only when government-backed entities' bottom line is of concern. Big name companies like Intel/Microsoft/Cisco have all been victims of that. In the long term, it actually helps these businesses to operate in a well-regulated economy, instead of having their engineers' hard work being ripped off to be sold pennies on the dollar or undercut their own businesses.

Was actually a fairly interesting problem that Kilby and Noyce solved. Engineers up until the point they decided to use Silicon were using multiple different materials that were optimal for the conductivity. Then both of them independently found that Silicon were perfectly adequate when doped with other elements, and radically simplified the production process of ICs. Suddenly the flood gates opened. Another interesting story, Japan spanked the living heck out of American companies by picking their battle when creating chips. They decided to out compete the US in memory production. At first there was denialism, until AMD came out with hard numbers to show that Japanese memory failed by an order of magnitude less than Japanese memory chips.
